<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="a8aec75daccb4c5ebb010d034380bf86"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a8aec75daccb4c5ebb010d034380bf86">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0c063657-033c-42d1-9201-9d47705ef49f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                s.src = url;

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d52356538" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon is regarded as a promising anode candidate for sodium-ion batteries&nbsp;due to its low cost, relatively low working voltage, and satisfactory specific capacity. However, it still remains a challenge to obtain a high-performance hard carbon anode from cost-effective carbon sources. In addition, the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) is subjected to continuous rupture during battery cycling, leading to fast capacity decay. Herein, a lignin-based hard carbon with robust SEI is developed to address these issues, effectively killing two birds with one stone. An innovative gas-phase removal-assisted aqueous washing strategy is developed to remove excessive sodium in the precursor to upcycle industrial lignin into high-value hard carbon, which demonstrated an ultrahigh sodium storage capacity of 359 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. It is found that the residual sodium components from lignin on hard carbon act as active sites that controllably regulate the composition and morphology of SEI and guide homogeneous SEI growth by a near-shore aggregation mechanism to form thin, dense, and organic-rich SEI. Benefiting from these merits, the as-developed SEI shows fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer at the interphases and enhanced structural stability, thus preventing SEI rupture and reformation, and ultimately leading to a comprehensive improvement in sodium storage performance.</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2538-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2538-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>The scarcity of lithium resources restricts further application of state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in large-scale energy storage systems, for which the top priority is no longer energy density but production cost.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0001_R_d52356530e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat Commun">1</a>, <a href="#cey2538-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0002_R_d52356530e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv Funct Mater">2</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which have analogous intercalation chemistry/electrochemistry as LIBs, are considered as promising alternatives to LIBs due to the abundance of sodium resources available.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0003_R_d52356530e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Science">3</a>-<a href="#cey2538-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0005_R_d52356530e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv Funct Mater">5</a></sup></span> The major issue impeding the commercialization of SIBs is the absence of appropriate anode materials. Thermodynamically, the commonly used graphite anodes in LIBs are not suitable in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0006_R_d52356530e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nature">6</a>-<a href="#cey2538-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0008_R_d52356530e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mol Cryst Liq Cryst">8</a></sup></span> In 2000, Stevens et al.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0009_R_d52356530e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J Electrochem Soc">9</a></sup></span> first reported that hard carbon obtained from glucose pyrolysis delivered reasonable reversible capacity in SIBs, which brought about a turnaround in the development of anode materials for SIBs. Since then, considerable efforts have been devoted toward the synthesis and optimization of hard carbon.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0010_R_d52356530e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mater Today">10</a>-<a href="#cey2538-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0014_R_d52356530e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv Mater">14</a></sup></span> Notably, the development of high-performance hard carbon with relatively low production costs is crucial for SIBs. Specifically, finding affordable carbon precursors is critical for the realization of low-cost hard carbon, while achievement of excellent electrochemical performance demands high sodium storage capacity and robust interphases to ensure long-term cycle stability.</p>
         
         <p>Biomass has gained attention as a significant feedstock category for the synthesis of hard carbon due to its economic viability and sustainability. However, the heterogeneity of feedstocks presents challenges to its large-scale production. Lignin, which is extracted from biomass, is the second most abundant organic material globally and is anticipated to offer a plethora of valuable hard carbon products.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0015_R_d52356530e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J Mater Res Technol">15</a></sup></span> Moreover, alkaline liquor (also known as black liquor), a byproduct derived from processes aimed at cellulose recovery from biomass, serves as an abundant source of industrial lignin. Unfortunately, the frequent utilization of sodium-containing chemicals during the alkaline pulping process for lignin separation from biomass leads to a high sodium content in the extracted industrial lignin (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0001">1A</a>), thereby significantly impeding its conversion into high-value-added carbon products.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0016_R_d52356530e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem Commun">16</a></sup></span> In fact, lignin-derived hard carbon shows unsatisfactory electrochemical performance, so it is necessary to blend lignin with other precursors such as pitch and polyacrylonitrile to improve the as-prepared hard carbon performance.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0017_R_d52356530e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J Mater Chem A">17</a>, <a href="#cey2538-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0018_R_d52356530e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a></sup></span> Therefore, the preparation of hard carbon with excellent sodium storage performance using industrial lignin remains a challenge. Considering previous studies reporting the use of residual sodium components on the cathode surface to establish a robust interphase,<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0019_R_d52356530e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv Mater">19</a></sup></span> there is great anticipation for the potential applicability of this concept in the negative electrode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2538-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6e160512-5c85-49ae-86ab-bbb00eca5d81/cey2538-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6e160512-5c85-49ae-86ab-bbb00eca5d81/cey2538-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2cbef2a3-9755-496c-a4dc-87ccd6a90741/cey2538-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6e160512-5c85-49ae-86ab-bbb00eca5d81/cey2538-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2538-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.538" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) Composition and macrostructures of the lignocellulose structure. (B) Weight loss curves for lignin. (C) TGA-FTIR data of lignin. (D) FTIR spectra of lignin pretreated at different temperatures. (E) Carbon yield of lignin pretreated at different temperatures.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In this study, we have successfully developed a hard carbon anode using industrial lignin. The as-developed hard carbon shows excellent electrochemical performance in SIBs in terms of reversible capacity and cycling life. Especially, a gas-phase removal-assisted aqueous washing strategy was proposed to remove sodium components inside the precursor to achieve high sodium storage capacity. Furthermore, it is found that residual sodium components on the as-synthesized hard carbon help to form robust solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers, which is beneficial for improving the anode performance. The comprehensive analysis of physical and chemical properties indicates that organic-rich SEI layers are uniformly and densely distributed on the surface of hard carbon. Theoretical calculations elucidate the mechanism underlying the formation of robust SEI, namely, the uniform nucleation deposition of SEI utilizing residual sodium components on the hard carbon surface via a near-shore aggregation mechanism. Hence, the utilization of tailored hard carbon materials, coupled with robust interphases, enables superior electrochemical performance of hard carbon anodes for SIBs. Specifically, a remarkable reversible capacity (359 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and 400 stable cycles are obtained.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2538-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2538-sec-0020-title">2 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2538-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2538-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis of lignin-derived hard carbon</h3>
            
            <p>In the global pulping and paper industry, lignin is produced as a byproduct of the pulping process at about 50 million tons annually.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0020_R_d52356530e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Green Chem">20</a></sup></span> Only a tiny percentage of this lignin is used for value-added products, and the remainder is commonly burned for electricity generation. To highlight the economy and universality of this study, the lignin used in the study was derived from alkali lignin from the pulp industry. Carbon yield is a key indicator for the preparation of hard carbon.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0021_R_d52356530e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv Energy Mater">21</a></sup></span> Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was first performed to determine the carbon yield of lignin pyrolysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0001">1B</a>), and it was found that the sample can achieve an approximately 40% carbon yield under an argon atmosphere at 1000°C. However, lignin remains as ∼20 wt% residual mass after being ashed up to 1000°C, which can be attributed to the oxidation products of the sodium components in lignin.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0022_R_d52356530e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Proc Combust Inst">22</a></sup></span> To determine the reasons for the low carbon yield (organic elemental analysis showed that lignin contained close to 51 wt% of carbon atoms), we used TGA coupled with online gas-phase Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR; Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0001">1C</a>,&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>) and gas chromatography–mass spectrometry (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>) to monitor the gas compositions during lignin thermal evolution. It was found that the sodium components of lignin can affect hard carbon yield by facilitating the release of small molecules and accelerating carbon decomposition (detailed analysis in Text&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>).</p>
            
            <p>To achieve higher carbon yields, we implemented a preoxidation strategy to enhance the cross-linking of lignin molecules, thereby inhibiting the release of small molecules during pyrolysis. The functional groups of lignin were monitored by FTIR during air preoxidation at different temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0001">1D</a>), and the results indicate that the degree of lignin cross-linking increases as the temperature increases.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0023_R_d52356530e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Materials">23</a></sup></span> Subsequently, the carbon yield of preoxidation lignin was assessed at different temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0001">1E</a>). The carbon yield showed an initial increase at lower temperatures with an increase in the pretreatment temperature, reaching a maximum value at 220°C, followed by a subsequent decrease at higher temperatures. This phenomenon can be attributed to the fact that preoxidation enhances the cross-linking between lignin molecules, thereby impeding rapid lignin decomposition during pyrolysis and ultimately increasing the yield. However, based on thermogravimetric curve analyses (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0001">1B</a>), excessive preoxidation at elevated temperatures may result in oxidative losses and consequently lead to reduced yields.</p>
            
            <p>Immediately following this, the synthesis condition was thoroughly investigated to achieve high-performance hard carbon. As shown in our results (see Text&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–S7</a>&nbsp;for more information), a high temperature of 1400°C is beneficial for the preparation of hard carbon. A gas-phase removal-assisted aqueous washing strategy was devised to eliminate the sodium components from hard carbon prepared from industrial lignin, based on the low boiling point (882.8°C) of the sodium and the good aqueous solubility of sodium salts. All the process flows are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0002">2A</a> (samples denote 700BW, 700AW, L-1400BW, L-1400AW, 1000BW, 1000AW, H-1400BW, and H-1400AW; BW = before washing, AW = after washing; L = low temperature, H = high temperature, and the numbers represent the temperature). The surface compositions and chemical states of elements in a series of lignin-derived carbon materials were investigated using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0002">2B</a>, detailed analysis in Text&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>). The XPS trend indicates that a higher temperature front-end heat treatment can expedite the migration of sodium to the material's surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0002">2C</a>), thereby facilitating its removal through the subsequent aqueous washing.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0024_R_d52356530e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Fuel Process Technol">24</a></sup></span> By implementing this strategy, we successfully achieved H-1400BW samples with significantly reduced surface sodium content, while the H-1400AW samples showed a complete absence of sodium components on their surfaces.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2538-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4fc5d7b0-19a7-4e46-b25a-c8487291c7e2/cey2538-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4fc5d7b0-19a7-4e46-b25a-c8487291c7e2/cey2538-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0e99a291-abd5-4666-9759-13773b29af72/cey2538-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4fc5d7b0-19a7-4e46-b25a-c8487291c7e2/cey2538-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2538-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.538" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) Preparation of hard carbon for SIBs using lignin by both high- and low-temperature pyrolysis routes at the front end, respectively. (B) XPS survey spectra of different samples. The right side of the graph shows the relative element contents calculated using XPS results. (C) Schematic diagram of sodium migration during lignin pyrolysis. (D) High-resolution Na 1s, O 1s, and C 1s XPS spectra of different samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To elucidate the specific form of sodium components on the surface of hard carbon and evaluate its suitability within the electrode material, the high-resolution Na 1s, O 1s, and C 1s spectra of lignin-derived materials are also examined (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0002">2D</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>). The binding energy at 1071 eV can be assigned to sodium compounds,<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0025_R_d52356530e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv Mater">25</a></sup></span> with their contents changing throughout the process steps, and the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> in C 1s and O 1s is also consistent with the changes in the sodium content. The X-ray&nbsp;diffraction patterns of the 1000BW and H-1400BW samples show that the gas-phase removal-assisted aqueous washing process effectively removes most of the sodium components and also confirm that the sodium components that migrate to the carbon surface are in the form of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>). It has been demonstrated that Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> is an important component of SEI membranes for SIBs,<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0026_R_d52356530e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv Funct Mater">26</a></sup></span> so the role of these sodium components remaining on the carbon surface in the electrochemical performance is highly anticipated. Furthermore, we also investigated the morphological characteristics of the prepared samples and further established the structural features of their hard carbon (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10–S12</a>). To sum up the observations from experimental characterizations, we successfully synthesized hard carbon samples from industrial lignin using a gas-phase removal-assisted aqueous washing process.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2538-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2538-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical testing and electrochemical kinetic analysis</h3>
            
            <p>In an attempt to evaluate the effect of the residual sodium components on electrochemistry and electrode kinetics, the half-cell configuration was used to investigate the capacity of hard carbons in sodium ion storage. The cycling performance and galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the H-1400BW and H-1400AW samples are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0003">3A,B</a>, demonstrating the exceptional sodium storage capabilities of lignin-derived hard carbon (H-1400BW: 359 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), which is one of the highest values based on previous publications (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>). The long low-potential plateau implies that most of the sodium ions are stored in the form of filled holes, which corresponds to the shoulder peaks observed by our small-angle X-ray scattering (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>). It is noteworthy that H-1400BW, which has a small number of sodium residues on the hard carbon surface, demonstrates improved cycling stability and capacity retention. Further charge–discharge curve analyses showed that both the sharp capacity drop at the beginning of the cycle and the capacity decay at the end of the cycle mainly occurred in the plateau region at low potential (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>), which is closely linked to the interfacial nature of the hard carbon surface. Therefore, the residual sodium components may play a key role in stabilizing the interphase and thus enhancing the electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2538-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e4a0058a-3724-4333-adbd-98e05609d1a2/cey2538-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>

CV curves of (F) H-1400BW and (G) H-1400AW at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> with surface control and diffusion control currents separated at each potential. (H) Capacitive contribution ratio of the H-1400BW electrode and the H-1400AW electrode in SIBs at scan rates ranging from 0.2 to 5.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To clarify the effect of the residual sodium components on the electrode kinetics, first, cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (scan rates [<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/48b83f41-d043-448e-b24d-901b08a372a5/cey2538-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0003" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $v$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>] of 0.1−5.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>) of the cells were constructed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0003">3C,D</a>). Both samples present similar electrochemical behaviors at various <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8887a7aa-256a-4e58-99cc-4c803b1099f1/cey2538-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0004" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0004.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $v$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, but H-1400BW has a relatively larger peak current (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b197cb5d-7399-46ee-88e8-253441916ef6/cey2538-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0005" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0005.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $i$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>), emphasizing its superior electrochemical kinetics. Next, the relationship between <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6b32ee25-6ad8-45b9-8aa7-06068f462c99/cey2538-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0006" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0006.png">

                  </semantics></math>, to examine sodium-storage behaviors of both anodes, where <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/33ee1bae-9d0e-4635-9a93-27834429b3d0/cey2538-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0009" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0009.png">

                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $b$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> are constants. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0003">3E</a> shows an excellent linear relationship between log(<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d7d6df89-1965-4d6c-8be1-985343cdef7d/cey2538-math-0011.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0011" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0011.png">

                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $v$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) and log(<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3ba73721-0abb-4f58-998b-adc9faa70489/cey2538-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0012" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0012.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $i$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>), yielding the calculated <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/485b2396-f1b8-42e7-a407-8092a2703aac/cey2538-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0013" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0013.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $b$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> values of 0.66 and 0.61 for H-1400BW and H-1400AW, respectively, and this suggests a combination of pseudocapacitive- and diffusive-controlled sodium-storage processes in H-1400BW and H-1400AW. The contribution of the two controls discussed above can be further calculated by the equation, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7340e473-6e9c-464b-b095-bb5ee5f24e43/cey2538-math-0014.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0014" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0014.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo>=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mfrac>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $i={k}_{1}v+{k}_{2}{v}^{\frac{1}{2}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/83d21ff5-5c63-4593-b09c-39ef22a0ea2c/cey2538-math-0015.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0015" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0015.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>

                        </mrow>

                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${k}_{1}v$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/10dc7689-8a14-47ee-ad89-933abcb1909d/cey2538-math-0016.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0016" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0016.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>

                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mfrac>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${k}_{2}{v}^{\frac{1}{2}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> correspond to the pseudocapacitance and diffusion contributions, respectively, where <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/99401719-a4db-4e2b-ac9a-bb6f5d73c75d/cey2538-math-0017.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0017" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0017.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>

                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                           </msub>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${k}_{1}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f34fc10e-9756-442a-a22e-2286609c3052/cey2538-math-0018.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0018" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0018.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${k}_{2}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> are constants), and the results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0003">3F−H</a>. At a <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/cd05bdcb-97c2-4b8c-a30c-a188835d3dee/cey2538-math-0019.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0019" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0019.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $v$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the pseudocapacitance of H-1400BW is calculated to be 44.8%, which is higher than that of H-1400AW (39.4%), and as <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/89bc7335-c82b-4fd3-adb5-02b5d8301e09/cey2538-math-0020.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2538:cey2538-math-0020" location="graphic/cey2538-math-0020.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $v$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> increases, the pseudocapacitive contribution of H-1400BW becomes more apparent. The high pseudocapacitance ratio indicates that the material favors surface redox reactions or that the electrode provides a favorable ion transport and adsorption path.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0027_R_d52356530e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv Energy Mater">27</a>, <a href="#cey2538-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0028_R_d52356530e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv Funct Mater">28</a></sup></span> The increased intercalation pseudocapacitance triggered by H-1400BW indicates faster diffusion and electron transport of sodium ions throughout the cell.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0029_R_d52356530e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Small">29</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>Considering that the distinguishing factor between the two electrodes lies in the presence of residual sodium components on the surface of hard carbon, our investigation next focuses on the electrochemical kinetics of interphases, as it plays a crucial role in ensuring consistent cycling performance of the cell.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0030_R_d52356530e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat Energy">30</a>, <a href="#cey2538-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0031_R_d52356530e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Solid State Ion">31</a></sup></span> Remarkably, the structural characteristics of the interphases are well reflected by the alternating current (AC) impedance.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0032_R_d52356530e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">32</a></sup></span> Nyquist plots of in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of H-1400BW and H-1400AW during discharge–charge processes are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0004">4A,B</a>. The charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of both in discharging is significantly higher than that in charging, which indicates that the resistance of the hard carbon material is significantly larger during sodium insertion than sodium desertion, due to the formation of SEI. It is worth noting that, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0004">4C</a>, H-1400BW shows a rapid reduction in <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> at the beginning of the discharge, with <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> reducing to a smaller value than H-1400AW during the first discharge. Moreover, the reactive sodium ion migration using the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) indicates that H-1400BW shows a relatively higher diffusion coefficient, indicative of the fast ion transport (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0004">4D,E</a>). Taken together, the robust interphase of H-1400BW is the key to improved cycling performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2538-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92cbedc4-c518-4d01-9818-f8d3fdb3fd05/cey2538-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/92cbedc4-c518-4d01-9818-f8d3fdb3fd05/cey2538-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/641d999b-d0da-4bda-ac89-ac67592c0c88/cey2538-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92cbedc4-c518-4d01-9818-f8d3fdb3fd05/cey2538-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2538-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2538-sec-0050-title">2.3 Fundamental insights into interfacial phenomena</h3>
            
            <p>After confirming that excellent electrochemical performance depends on robust interphases, the remaining issue is the impact of the residual sodium components on the physical and chemical properties of SEI. First, we investigated the composition and content of SEI on hard carbon surfaces. Time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) for the H-1400BW electrode and the H-1400AW electrode after the first cycle was performed in the range of positive polarity. Analysis of a large number of SEI fragments from the H-1400BW electrode and the H-1400AW electrode indicates considerable similarity in their SEI composition&nbsp;(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0005">5A</a>), with SEI fragments mainly assigned to sodium-containing inorganic contents (62 [Na<sub>2</sub>O<sup>+</sup>], 63 [Na<sub>2</sub>OH<sup>+</sup>], 65 [Na<sub>2</sub>F<sup>+</sup>], 85 [Na<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>], 107 [Na<sub>3</sub>F<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>], and 129 [Na<sub>3</sub>CO<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup>]).<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0026_R_d52356530e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv Funct Mater">26</a></sup></span> Notably, there were considerable differences in these inorganic contents. The changes in SEI can be attributed to the presence of residual sodium components on the surface of the hard carbon. Otherwise, the morphology of SEI on both samples was found to be different in terms of structure and thickness when we compared their transmission electron microscopy&nbsp;images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0005">5B</a>). The SEI of H-1400BW is relatively thin and dense, with a uniform film layer, while that of H-1400AW has a hemispherical pile-up on the material's surface.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2538-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f16c7bcf-62d4-4ce4-95f8-449c3e51ac65/cey2538-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f16c7bcf-62d4-4ce4-95f8-449c3e51ac65/cey2538-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cde85938-4927-4777-8646-2498c1390570/cey2538-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f16c7bcf-62d4-4ce4-95f8-449c3e51ac65/cey2538-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2538-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.538" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) TOF-SIMS spectra for the H-1400BW electrode and the H-1400AW electrode after the first cycle. (B) High-resolution transmission electron microscopy image of the H-1400BW electrode and the H-1400AW electrode after the first cycle. (C) Variation of the atomic content of the H-1400BW electrode and the H-1400AW electrode after the first cycle measured by XPS at different sputtering thicknesses. (D) F 1s XPS spectra of the H-1400BW electrode and the H-1400AW electrode. (E) Schematic pictures of SEI formation processes based on the near-shore aggregation mechanism. (F) Simulated interaction configurations and (G) calculated adsorption energy between the nucleation center (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) and the main SEI components of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Besides content and morphology, the spatial distribution of SEI components also has a profound impact on the cycling stability of electrodes. To understand the effect of residual sodium components, Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering was used to probe different depths of the SEI layer. With an increase in the etching depth, the inner SEI of H-1400BW contains more sodium and fluorine components with fewer carbon and oxygen contents than that of H-1400AW (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0005">5C</a>). In particular, the F 1s XPS spectra show that the NaF content in the SEI of H-1400BW increases significantly with increasing etching depth (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0005">5D</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0033_R_d52356530e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J Electrochem Soc">33</a>, <a href="#cey2538-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0034_R_d52356530e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew Chem Int Ed">34</a></sup></span> Previous studies have demonstrated that inorganic NaF-rich SEI layers are more resistant to stress changes during cycling, thus resulting in better structural stability.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0035_R_d52356530e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew Chem">35</a></sup></span> In addition, the high-resolution C 1s XPS spectra show relatively lower contents of alkyl carbonate and sodium carbonate and higher contents of organic carbon in the SEI of H-1400BW (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0027_R_d52356530e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv Energy Mater">27</a>, <a href="#cey2538-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0036_R_d52356530e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J Alloys Compd">36</a></sup></span> Based on this, it can be deduced that H-1400BW is enriched with inorganic NaF and organic components of SEI to ensure its long-cycle stability, with all of the performance–structure differences attributed to the reconstruction of the SEI due to the transition of sodium residues to integrate SEI compositions.</p>
            
            <p>Considering the key role that residual sodium components play in hard carbon, we further investigated the underlying mechanism. For this, we analyzed typical patterns in the formation of SEI. Two typical growth mechanisms for SEI have been reported: the nanoscale SEI “surface growth” model, which combines classical molecular dynamics and Monte Carlo methods,<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0037_R_d52356530e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Phys Chem C">37</a></sup></span> and the “near-shore aggregation” mechanism reported by Ushirogata et al.<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0038_R_d52356530e1026" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J Electrochem Soc">38</a></sup></span> For the mechanism of “near-shore aggregation,” it is proposed that the SEI components formed at the electrode surface desorb into the near-shore region and aggregate, subsequently coalescing and contacting the electrode to complete SEI formation. Given the higher solubility of SEI in SIBs,<span><sup><a href="#cey2538-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0039_R_d52356530e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Energy Lett">39</a>, <a href="#cey2538-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2538-bib-0040_R_d52356530e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew Chem Int Ed">40</a></sup></span> it can be inferred that the growth mechanism of SEI in SIBs is more inclined towards near-shore aggregation, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0005">5E</a>. As a result of the residual sodium salt component on the hard carbon surface, we may infer that the interphase of H-1400BW is nucleation-based growth, resulting in a thin and dense SEI. The H-1400AW sample, on the other hand, lacks a nucleation center and the SEI growth is dominated by nuclei growth, which in turn leads to hemispherical large particles with uneven SEI components.</p>
            
            <p>To further validate our proposed hypothesis, a density functional theory&nbsp;calculation is performed. We performed simulation of the interactions between residual salt components (e.g., Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) on the surface of the material and the main SEI components (e.g., Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF as detected by XPS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0005">5F</a>). The calculated adsorption energies of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF inorganics on the Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> slab show that the residual surface Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> has a high affinity for both Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF components (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2538-fig-0005">5G</a>), indicating that the sodium salts remaining on the hard carbon surface interact strongly with the SEI membrane components dissolved in the electrolyte. This will promote SEI nucleation and uniform growth. The computational results adequately account for the experimental observations and clarify the real SEI growth behavior in our studied system.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2538-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2538-sec-0060-title">3 CONCLUSIONS</h2>
         
         <p>Lignin-derived hard carbon was synthesized using a gas-phase removal-assisted aqueous washing process. The as-synthesized hard carbon anode shows remarkable sodium storage performance on evaluation as an anode material for SIBs. One of the key findings to emerge from this study is that the residual sodium components on the hard carbon surface play an important role in SEI formation; specifically, they can modulate both the composition and morphology of the SEI, thereby yielding robust interphases. Theoretical calculations have verified our finding and confirmed that migration of sodium carbonate to the hard carbon surface helps to promote nucleation and homogeneous growth of SEI. These findings are crucial for comprehending the process of synthesizing high-performance hard carbon and elucidating the formation and regulation mechanisms of SEI in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2538-sec-0070-title"> ACKNOWLEDGMENTS</h2>

CX20220183). Simin Li thanks the National Natural Science Foundation of China (Grant no. 52204327).</p>
